        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b21213"><meta property="og:title" content="Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP2 Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/acsami.8b21213.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are more feasible for grid-scale applications than their lithium-ion counterparts when abundant sodium resources with an even geographic distribution are taken into consideration. However, developing an anode presents a major challenge since the standard graphite anode shows a limited Na-ion storage capacity. Here we report a CuCl2/chitosan monolith derived CuP2/C composite where CuP2 nanoparticles are uniformly embedded in the carbon matrix. The strong chemical bonding between electron rich groups in chitosan and the heavy metal ion (Cu2+) plays a key role for the synthesis of the homogeneous monolithic composite, and chitosan derived carbon prevents Cu and CuP2 particles from aggregation upon the following thermal reduction and phosphorization. Benefiting from the synergistic effect of small particle size and conductive carbon matrix, the CuP2/C composite, as an anode for SIBs, delivers a high reversible capacity of 630 mAh/g at a current density of 100 mA/g and a capacity retention ratio of 91% after 200 cycles, while bare CuP2 shows a rapid capacity decay within 50 cycles."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/acsami.8b21213.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318896e9a4c54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318896e9a4c54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '38ad9e4d-59a4-4a6c-afb5-6d2ec0bb086d'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318896e9a4c54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318896e9a4c54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Chitosan Derived Carbon Matrix Encapsulated CuP&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Nanoparticles for Sodium-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b21213", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">40</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/medium/am-2018-21213r_0001.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b21213&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are more feasible for grid-scale applications than their lithium-ion counterparts when abundant sodium resources with an even geographic distribution are taken into consideration. However, developing an anode presents a major challenge since the standard graphite anode shows a limited Na-ion storage capacity. Here we report a CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith derived CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite where CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are uniformly embedded in the carbon matrix. The strong chemical bonding between electron rich groups in chitosan and the heavy metal ion (Cu<sup>2+</sup>) plays a key role for the synthesis of the homogeneous monolithic composite, and chitosan derived carbon prevents Cu and CuP<sub>2</sub> particles from aggregation upon the following thermal reduction and phosphorization. Benefiting from the synergistic effect of small particle size and conductive carbon matrix, the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite, as an anode for SIBs, delivers a high reversible capacity of 630 mAh/g at a current density of 100 mA/g and a capacity retention ratio of 91% after 200 cycles, while bare CuP<sub>2</sub> shows a rapid capacity decay within 50 cycles.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D12415%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJian%2BDuan%252C%2BShengyuan%2BDeng%252C%2BWangyan%2BWu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D13%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b21213%26title%3DChitosan%2BDerived%2BCarbon%2BMatrix%2BEncapsulated%2BCuP2%2BNanoparticles%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorage%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D12420%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290975&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Biopolymers"> Biopolymers </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon"> Carbon </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles"> Nanoparticles </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Chitosan&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_chitosan">chitosan</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Matrix&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20matrix">carbon matrix</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cup2+Nanoparticles&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_CuP2%20nanoparticles">CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Monolith&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_monolith">monolith</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have become the default choice for portable electronics since their commercialization in 1991 and are growing in popularity for transportation and grid energy storage applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> As electric vehicles become popular, the demand for lithium resources increases sharply and questions about alternatives of LIBs are timely. Much greater natural abundance of sodium resources (by a factor of 10<sup>3</sup>) and similar intercalation chemistry to LIBs make sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) promising substitutes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 4 to 7">(4−7)</a> Unfortunately, commercially available graphite shows a significantly low capacity as an SIB anode. Great efforts for exploiting available anodes have been devoted to amorphous carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 8 to 12">(8−12)</a> alloy-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a> transition metal sulfides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 16 to 21">(16−21)</a> and metal oxides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> As another option, phosphorus (P) shows the highest theoretical sodium storage capacity and relatively low desodiation potential (∼0.6 V). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24−26)</a> However, the challenges for its wide application are enormous. P possesses a low electrical conductivity and poses large volume expansion (∼290%, by molar volume) upon sodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> The related volume change would cause large anisotropic stresses within the electrode, fracturing and crumbling P particles, which result in a rapid capacity decay.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, P-based composites or phosphides have attracted great attention due to their high electric conductivity and much better cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 29 to 34">(29−34)</a> Among them, CuP<sub>2</sub> gives a high theoretical capacity of 1282 mAh/g based on the conversion and alloy reaction with Na: CuP<sub>2</sub> + 6Na ↔ Cu + 2Na<sub>3</sub>P. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 to 37">(35−37)</a> Previous works have demonstrated that designing nanostructured CuP<sub>2</sub> and engineering a conductive framework could promote the electrochemical performance of CuP<sub>2</sub>. For example, Manthiram and Kim chemically bonded CuP<sub>2</sub> and C by high energy mechanical milling red P, Cu, and acetylene black carbon. It was demonstrated that the carbon network functioned as a durable matrix in enhancing electrode conductivity and accommodating large volume changes during repeated cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Most recently, a cross-linking hollow carbon sheet was employed to control particle size of CuP<sub>2</sub> and stabilize the porous structure, which can eliminate the negative effects from the large volume change of CuP<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Although progress has been made in reducing particle size of CuP<sub>2</sub> and adding carbon, it is still a major challenge to develop a scalable approach for practical application.</div><div class="NLM_p">Here we develop a convenient method to fabricate a CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite with CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded in the carbon matrix using chitosan, the second most abundant polysaccharide in nature, as Cu<sup>2+</sup> absorber and carbon sources. Chitosan has a strong ability to absorb heavy metal ions and is easily fabricated in monolithic materials with hierarchical porous structures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 40 to 43">(40−43)</a> Inspired by this, we obtain a three-dimensional (3D) porous CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith by freeze drying the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan solution, in which Cu<sup>2+</sup> ions are adhered on chitosan. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, thermal reduction turns the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith to the Cu/C composite, and the following phosphorization process gives the final CuP<sub>2</sub>/C product. More importantly, chitosan derived carbon effectively prevents particle aggregation and maintains the 3D porous structure upon the two-step treatment. The CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite delivers a high Na-ion storage capacity of 630 mAh/g at 100 mA/g, excellent rate capability with a specific capacity of 413 mAh/g at 400 mA/g, and superior cycling stability with a capacity retention of 91% after 200 cycles. Furthermore, full cells have been assembled by pairing the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, which show a capacity retention of 90% over 100 cycles.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/medium/am-2018-21213r_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic of the synthesis process of CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite. Freeze drying CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan solution gives the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith, which is transformed into the Cu/C monolith via thermal reduction. Subsequently, the Cu/C composite is phosphorizated to obtain the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C product where Cu nanoparticles are in situ transformed to CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded in the chitosan derived carbon matrix.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b21213&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Materials Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To prepare the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite, the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith is fabricated via freeze drying the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan solution with 0.2 g of chitosan and 0.5 g of copper chloride dihydrate in 40 mL of deionized water. The monolith is thermally treated at 500 °C for 4 h in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (5%) to obtain the Cu/C composite. The final CuP<sub>2</sub>/C product is obtained by heating a Cu/C and red P mixture in a sealed stainless-steel vial at 425 °C for 6 h. Bare CuP<sub>2</sub> is synthesized by heating Cu with red P at 425 °C for 6 h. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> powder is synthesized via a one-step solid-state method using Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and glucose as precursors according to our present study.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Characterizations and Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p">The phase information is checked by X-ray diffraction (XRD, PANalytical B.V., Holland). The morphology is observed by a scanning electron microscope coupled with an energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) detector (FEI, Q250, USA). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observation is carried out on a JEOL 2100F microscope. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) is performed on a TENSOR II (Bruker Company, Germany) in the wavenumber range of 400 to 4000 cm<sup>–1</sup> at room temperature by an average of 16 scans with a resolution of 4 cm<sup>–1</sup> in transmission mode. TG/DTA analysis is performed in air on a Pyris 1 TGA (PerkinElmer Instruments). N<sub>2</sub> sorption measurement is performed on a Quantachrome Autosorb-iQ gas sorptometer.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performances are tested with 2032 coin cells. For sodium-ion half cell evaluation, sodium foil is used as both counter and reference electrodes, 1 M solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and polypropylene carbonate (PC) (1:1 by volume) with addition of 5 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte, and Whatman glass fiber as the separator. The CuP<sub>2</sub>/C or bare CuP<sub>2</sub> electrode is prepared by casting a slurry of 80 wt % active material, 10 wt % super P, and 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) onto copper foil and then dried in a vacuum oven at 80 °C for 24 h. The active mass loading in the electrode is around 2 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>. For full cells, the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C electrode was used as the anode, and the NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) electrode was used as the cathode. The NVP electrode is prepared by casting a slurry of 80 wt % active material, 10 wt % super P, and 10 wt % PVDF onto aluminum foil, followed by 80 °C drying. The electrolyte and separator used are the aforementioned ones of the sodium-ion half cell. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests are carried out using a battery tester (Neware, China) at 25 °C. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) is done on an electrochemical workstation (Biologic VMP3).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows a digital image of the 3D porous CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith after freeze drying, where the sublimation of solvent left a large number of pores in the monolith. By the following thermal treatment in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (5%), the monolith structure is maintained well while the color changes from yellow (CuCl<sub>2</sub>) to pinkish-orange (metallic Cu), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. The XRD pattern of the Cu/C monolith indicates that CuCl<sub>2</sub> is thermally reduced to metallic Cu while chitosan acts as a carbon source to enable a Cu/C composite (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/suppl_file/am8b21213_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The content of carbon in the composite is calculated to be 30.4%, as characterized by TG/DTA (see more details in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/suppl_file/am8b21213_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). Interestingly, the following phosphorization process does not affect the overall monolith morphology (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). XRD results confirm the effective phosphorization that Cu is converted to CuP<sub>2</sub> with a monoclinic phase (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/medium/am-2018-21213r_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0005.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Digital images of (a) the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan, (b) Cu/C, and (c) CuP<sub>2</sub>/C monoliths. The 3D porous structure is maintained well upon the thermal reduction and phosphorization. (d) XRD pattern of CuP<sub>2</sub>/C monolith (inset is structure of CuP<sub>2</sub>). (e) SEM image of CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith after freeze drying. SEM and corresponding EDX images of (f–i) Cu/C and (j–m) CuP<sub>2</sub>/C monoliths.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b21213&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A SEM image of the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith exhibits a porous structure consisting of CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan sheets (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). After the following thermal reduction process, the Cu/C composite maintains a porous monolithic structure, and Cu/C sheets several microns thick interconnect with each other. Surprisingly, there is no metallic Cu particle aggregation on the surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f,g). The corresponding EDX images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>h,i show a well distributed Cu on the whole carbon sheet, indicating chitosan derived carbon plays a key role in preventing Cu particles from aggregation. Elemental nitrogen (N) is also detected since chitosan is a linear polysaccharide, which is composed of a substantial number of acetylated units (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/suppl_file/am8b21213_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). According to previous reports, N-doped carbon derived from chitosan has a high conductivity, indicating great promise for battery applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The phosphorization process is conducted just above the sublimation temperature of red phosphorus to realize the successful transformation of Cu nanoparticles to CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, which disperse uniformly on the carbon sheets without particle aggregation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>j–m). The N<sub>2</sub> sorption–desorption isotherm of CuP<sub>2</sub> is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/suppl_file/am8b21213_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> with the calculated BET surface area of 188.1 m<sup>2</sup>/g.</div><div class="NLM_p">TEM observation is used to capture fine details of the Cu/C and CuP<sub>2</sub>/C monoliths. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, Cu nanoparticles are uniformly embedded in carbon nanosheets of the Cu/C composite. The particle size of Cu is around 4 nm without aggregation due to the restriction of the chitosan derived carbon matrix. Lattice fringes with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.209 nm can be detected, which corresponds to the (1 1 1) planes of Cu (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). After the phosphorization process, Cu nanoparticles transform into CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded in the carbon matrix (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). The high-resolution TEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d further presents lattice fringes of CuP<sub>2</sub> with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.289 nm, corresponding to the (−1 1 2) plane of CuP<sub>2</sub>. Here, we succeed in fabricating the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite, of which CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are homogeneously embedded in the carbon matrix.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/medium/am-2018-21213r_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Low-magnification TEM and HRTEM images of (a,b) Cu/C composite and (c,d) CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite. Cu nanoparticles with an average particle size of 4 nm are phosphorizated into CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles where the chitosan derived carbon matrix plays a critical role in limiting the particle aggregation upon thermal reduction and phosphorization.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b21213&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The formation mechanism of the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite is further investigated. Typically, chitosan, produced commercially by deacetylation of chitin, consists of protophilic organic groups, such as C═O, N–H, C–O, and C–N, which offer chitosan the strong ability to absorb metal ions (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/suppl_file/am8b21213_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The FTIR spectrum of chitosan in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> exhibits two obvious absorbance peaks at 1628.15 and 1518.49 cm<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to the C═O stretch of the amide bond and N–H bending vibration of secondary amide, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The corresponding peaks in the FTIR spectrum of the CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith shift to lower wavenumbers (1589.54 and 1516.74 cm<sup>–1</sup>), which is attributed to the electrostatic interactions between Cu<sup>2+</sup> and protophilic organic groups of chitosan. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The peaks of chitosan located at 1382.69 and 1089.36 cm<sup>–1</sup>, due to C–N and C–O, respectively, also shift to lower wavenumbers after the adsorption of CuCl<sub>2</sub>. The FTIR results reveal that −OH, C═O, N–H, C–N, and C–O groups in chitosan are the active sites for Cu<sup>2+</sup> absorption. The strong interaction between Cu<sup>2+</sup> and chitosan ensures homogeneous mixing and prevents CuCl<sub>2</sub> from becoming large particles during the freeze drying process. During the following thermal treatment and phosphorization, Cu<sup>2+</sup> ions are in situ reduced to Cu nanoparticles and converted to CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, while chitosan becomes carbon and restricts the growth of Cu and CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/medium/am-2018-21213r_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. FTIR spectra of chitosan (black line) and CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith (blue line). Compared to bare chitosan, electrostatic interactions between the heavy metal ions (Cu<sup>2+</sup>) and electron rich groups in chitosan cause the change in the bond length, leading to peak shift.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b21213&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite is evaluated in 2032-type half cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the CV curves for the initial three cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV/s. There are two broad reduction waves around 0.35 and 0.01 V of the cathodic sweep, which are ascribed to the multistep sodiation of CuP<sub>2</sub> to Cu and Na<sub>3</sub>P. In the ensuing anodic sweep, two broad waves appear at 0.65 and 0.88 V, showing a reversible desodiation process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> It is noticed that the current in the first CV cycle is smaller than the following cycles, suggestive of an activation process since CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are well embedded in the carbon matrix. After the first cycle, CV curves overlap and remain steady, showing great stability.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/medium/am-2018-21213r_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0002.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curves for the first three cycles of CuP<sub>2</sub>/C electrode in half cell. (b) Rate capability and Coulombic efficiency of CuP<sub>2</sub>/C electrode in half cell at different current rates. (c) Cycling performance of CuP<sub>2</sub>/C and bare CuP<sub>2</sub> electrodes at 200 mA/g. (d) Typical charge/discharge voltage profiles of the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C and NVP electrodes in half cells at 0.2 C. (e) Voltage profile of the full cell (NVP|CuP<sub>2</sub>/C) at 0.2 C (1 C = 100 mA/g). (f) Cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of the full cell at 0.4 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/asset/images/large/am-2018-21213r_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b21213&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite electrode exhibits an excellent rate capability with capacities of 630, 519, 413, and 263 mAh/g at current densities of 100, 200, 400, and 800 mA/g, respectively. The specific capacities and current densities are calculated based on the total mass of the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite. When the current density is rested to 100 mA/g, the capacity recovers to 626 mAh/g. The excellent rate capability is due to the good combination of CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and carbon matrix. The CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite also shows a stable cycle performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). At the beginning, the capacity increases slightly in few cycles, which is due to the activation process, agreeing well with the CV results. After 200 cycles, the capacity maintains a specific capacity of 477 mAh/g, giving a capacity retention of 91%. We also prepare bare CuP<sub>2</sub> by phosphorization of Cu mesh as a control. The bare CuP<sub>2</sub> electrode delivers a slightly higher capacity at the beginning. However, the capacity fades rapidly from 625 to 95 mAh/g within 50 cycles. As known, the large volume change of CuP<sub>2</sub> upon electrochemical cycling results in electrode pulverization and electrical contact loss, leading to the fast capacity decay. On the other hand, the carbon matrix in the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite acts as a buffer to accommodate the volume change and enables the electronic pathway at the same time. In addition, the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite shows high specific lithium-ion storage capacity (see more details in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/suppl_file/am8b21213_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">We further paired the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite with the NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode to investigate the applicability of CuP<sub>2</sub>/C in full cells. NVP is prepared according to our previous study (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b21213/suppl_file/am8b21213_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S7–S9</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> According to the average working potentials of 3.4 and 0.8 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na for the NVP cathode and the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C anode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d), the full cell operates at around 2.6 V, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. The full cell shows an excellent cycle stability with a 90.6% capacity retention and nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency over 100 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f). The excellent cycling stability is ascribed to superior cycle stability of both the NVP cathode and CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite anode.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have taken advantage of chitosan’s high adsorption activity and facile fabrication of monolithic materials to synthesize the CuP<sub>2</sub>/C composite via freeze drying, thermal reduction, and phosphorization. CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are uniformly embedded in the chitosan derived carbon matrix, which exhibit a high reversible capacity, superior rate capacity, and stable cycling performance, with 91% capacity retention after 200 cycles. A full cell of NVP and CuP<sub>2</sub>/C also exhibits stable cycling performance. We demonstrate that chitosan’s absorption capability for Cu<sup>2+</sup> enables the homogeneous CuCl<sub>2</sub>/chitosan monolith and the chitosan derived carbon matrix acts as an agent for limiting the growth and aggregation of Cu and CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles during thermal treatment. Moreover, the carbon matrix can accommodate the volume change and maintain an electron pathway for CuP<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles upon the electrochemical charge/discharge process. We believe that our methodology is potentially competitive for the practical production of nanosized metal phosphides and could be extensively applied to other metal-based electrode materials for battery applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b21213" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b21213</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern of the Cu/C monolith;         </div><div class="casAbstract">This review covers key technol. developments and scientific challenges for a broad range of Li-ion battery electrodes.  Periodic table and potential/capacity plots are used to compare many families of suitable materials.  Performance characteristics, current limitations, and recent breakthroughs in the development of com. intercalation materials such as lithium cobalt oxide (LCO), lithium nickel cobalt manganese oxide (NCM), lithium nickel cobalt aluminum oxide (NCA), lithium iron phosphate (LFP), lithium titanium oxide (LTO) and others are contrasted with that of conversion materials, such as alloying anodes (Si, Ge, Sn, etc.), chalcogenides (S, Se, Te), and metal halides (F, Cl, Br, I).  New polyanion cathode materials are also discussed.  The cost, abundance, safety, Li and electron transport, volumetric expansion, material dissoln., and surface reactions for each type of electrode materials are described.  Both general and specific strategies to overcome the current challenges are covered and categorized.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C3EE43182G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, H.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Progress in flexible lithium batteries and future prospects</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1307</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1338</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Progress in flexible lithium batteries and future prospects</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Guangmin; Li, Feng; Cheng, Hui-Ming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the advent of flexible electronics, flexible lithium-ion batteries have attracted great attention as a promising power source in the emerging field of flexible and wearable electronic devices such as roll-up displays, touch screens, conformable active radio-frequency identification tags, wearable sensors and implantable medical devices.  In this review, we summarize the recent research progress of flexible lithium-ion batteries, with special emphasis on electrode material selectivity and battery structural design.  We begin with a brief introduction of flexible lithium-ion batteries and the current development of flexible solid-state electrolytes for applications in this field.  This is followed by a detailed overview of the recent progress on flexible electrode materials based on carbon nanotubes, graphene, carbon cloth, conductive paper (cellulose), textiles and some other low-dimensional nanostructured materials.  Then recently proposed prototypes of flexible cable/wire type, transparent and stretchable lithium-ion batteries are highlighted.  The latest advances in the exploration of other flexible battery systems such as lithium-sulfur, Zn-C (MnO2) and sodium-ion batteries, as well as related electrode materials are included.  Finally, the prospects and challenges toward the practical uses of flexible lithium-ion batteries in electronic devices are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C5CS00362H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanostructured conductive polymers for advanced energy storage</span>. <i>Chem. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>44</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">6684</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6696</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C5CS00362H</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D44%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D6684-6696%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DY.%2BShi%26author%3DL.%2BPeng%26author%3DY.%2BDing%26author%3DY.%2BZhao%26author%3DG.%2BYu%26title%3DNanostructured%2Bconductive%2Bpolymers%2Bfor%2Badvanced%2Benergy%2Bstorage%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC5CS00362H&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b21213&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC5CS00362H&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanostructured conductive polymers for advanced energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shi, Ye; Peng, Lele; Ding, Yu; Zhao, Yu; Yu, Guihua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6684-6696</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Conductive polymers combine the attractive properties assocd. with conventional polymers and unique electronic properties of metals or semiconductors.  Recently, nanostructured conductive polymers have aroused considerable research interest owing to their unique properties over their bulk counterparts, such as large surface areas and shortened pathways for charge/mass transport, which make them promising candidates for broad applications in energy conversion and storage, sensors, actuators, and biomedical devices.  Numerous synthetic strategies have been developed to obtain various conductive polymer nanostructures, and high-performance devices based on these nanostructured conductive polymers have been realized.  This Tutorial review describes the synthesis and characteristics of different conductive polymer nanostructures; presents the representative applications of nanostructured conductive polymers as active electrode materials for electrochem. capacitors and lithium-ion batteries and new perspectives of functional materials for next-generation high-energy batteries, meanwhile discusses the general design rules, advantages, and limitations of nanostructured conductive polymers in the energy storage field; and provides new insights into future directions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201702648"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Metal–Organic Framework-Derived Materials for Sodium Energy Storage</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1702648</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Hierarchical Vesicular Carbon Co-Doped with S, P, N for High-Rate Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zou Guoqiang; Hou Hongshuai; Guo Tianxiao; Jiang Yunling; Ji Xiaobo; Zou Guoqiang; Hou Hongshuai; Guo Tianxiao; Jiang Yunling; Ji Xiaobo; Foster Christopher W; Banks Craig E; Zhang Yun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1800241</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Hierarchical nanoscale carbons have received wide interest as electrode materials for energy storage and conversion due to their fast mass transfer processes, outstanding electronic conductivity, and high stability.  Here, heteroatom (S, P, and N) doped hierarchical vesicular carbon (HHVC) materials with a high surface area up to 867.5 m(2) g(-1) are successfully prepared using a surface polymerization of hexachloro-cyclotriphosphazene (HCCP) and 4,4'-sulfonyldiphenol (BPS) on the ZIF-8 polyhedrons.  Significantly, it is the first time to achieve a controllability of the wall thickness for this unique carbon, ranging from 18 to 52 nm.  When utilized as anodes for sodium ion batteries, these novel carbon materials exhibit a high specific capacity of 327.2 mAh g(-1) at 100 mA g(-1) after 100 cycles, which can be attributed to the expanded interlayer distance and enhanced conductivity derived from the doping of heteroatoms.  Importantly, a high capacity of 142.6 mAh g(-1) can be obtained even at a high current density of 5 A g(-1), assigning to fast ion/electronic transmission processes stemming from the unique hierarchical vesicular structure.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nitrogen-rich hierarchically porous carbon as a high-rate anode material with ultra-stable cyclability and high capacity for capacitive sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Xudong; Sun, Xiaohong; Yoo, Seung Joon; Evanko, Brian; Fan, Fengru; Cai, Shu; Zheng, Chunming; Hu, Wenbin; Stucky, Galen D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">56</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon-based anode materials hold a promising future for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their natural abundance and low cost of development.  In spite of carbon's important role in the commercialization of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), further exploration is necessary in order to find high-performance, high-rate carbon anode materials for SIBs.  A honeycomb-like, nitrogen-rich (17.72 at%), hierarchically porous, and highly disordered carbonaceous material (N-HC) with an expanded interlayer distance (0.44 nm in av.) is synthesized by spray drying and subsequent pyrolysis under flowing NH3.  The hierarchically porous structure and rich nitrogen doping result in a large sp. surface area (722 m2 g-1), more defects and active sites, and greater functional interface accessibility for the active porous carbonaceous material and electrolyte.  When N-HC is used as the anode material for SIBs, the batteries display favorable discharge capacities (255.9 mA h g-1 in the 3000th cycle at 500 mA g-1) and good capacitive-energy-storage behavior (67% at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s-1) with excellent high-rate performance and ultra-stable cyclability over 10,000 cycles at 5000 mA g-1.  Our results show that the combination of the hierarchically porous structure and nitrogen doping leads to improved energy storage by increasing the capacitive energy storage, which enhances the high-rate performance of N-HC.  To further enhance the performance of the material, an elec. pretreatment is employed to increase the initial Coulombic efficiency of N-HC to 79.5%, a record high for an SIB cell.  A full cell with an N-HC anode and a Na3V2(PO4)3/C cathode shows a high capacity with a favorable cyclability (238.7 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at 100 mA g-1 and a capacity retention of 95.3% compared to the second cycle).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/nn4025674"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrospun Sb/C Fibers for a Stable and Fast Sodium-Ion Battery Anode</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">6378</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6386</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a top alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of Na-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.  However, the development of anode materials for SIBs to date was mainly limited to carbonaceous materials with minimal research devoted to high capacity alloy-based materials.  An Sb/C electrode with ∼30 nm Sb nanoparticles (NPs) uniformly encapsulated in interconnecting 1-dimensional (1D) 400 nm C fibers (denoted as SbNP@C) was fabricated using a simple and scalable electrospinning method. The SbNP@C electrode showed an initial total capacity of 422 mA-h/gelectrode and retained 350 mA-h/gelectrode after 300 deep charge-discharge cycles under 100 mA/gSb.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sb-C nanofibers with long cycle life as an anode material for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Lin; Hu, Xiaohong; Qian, Jiangfeng; Pei, Feng; Wu, Fayuan; Mao, Rongjun; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi; Cao, Yuliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb-C nanofibers are synthesized successfully through a single-nozzle electrospinning technique and subsequent calcination.  The structural and morphol. characterizations reveal the uniform nanofiber structure with the Sb nanoparticles embedded homogeneously in the carbon nanofibers.  Electrochem. expts. show that the Sb-C nanofiber electrode can deliver large reversible capacity (631 mA h g-1) at C/15, greatly improved rate capability (337 mA h g-1 at 5 C) and excellent cycling stability (90% capacity retention after 400 cycles).
